</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Kebatinan" class="mw-redirect" title="Kebatinan">Kebatinan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Javanese_sacred_places" title="Javanese sacred places">Javanese sacred places</a></div>
<p>Today, most Javanese follow <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> as their religion,<sup id="cite_ref-religion_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-religion-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> while only 5-10 percent of Javanese follow orthodox Islamic traditions.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Orthodox Muslims are most common in the northern coast bordering the <a href="/wiki/Java_Sea" title="Java Sea">Java Sea</a>, where Islam was first brought to the island. Islam first came in contact with Java during the Majapahit period, when they traded or made tributary relations with various states like Perlak and <a href="/wiki/Samudra_Pasai" class="mw-redirect" title="Samudra Pasai">Samudra Pasai</a> in modern-day <a href="/wiki/Aceh" title="Aceh">Aceh</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-wink_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wink-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A minority of Javanese also follow <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> (<a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestantism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Catholicism" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholicism">Catholicism</a>), which are concentrated in <a href="/wiki/Central_Java" title="Central Java">Central Java</a> (particularly <a href="/wiki/Surakarta" title="Surakarta">Surakarta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Magelang" title="Magelang">Magelang</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yogyakarta" title="Yogyakarta">Yogyakarta</a> for Catholicism). Native Christian churches such as the <a href="/wiki/Javanese_Christian_Church" title="Javanese Christian Church">Gereja Kristen Jawa</a> also exist. On a smaller scale, <a href="/wiki/Buddhism_in_Indonesia" title="Buddhism in Indonesia">Buddhism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hinduism_in_Indonesia" title="Hinduism in Indonesia">Hinduism</a> are also found in the Javanese community. The Javanese of the <a href="/wiki/Tenggerese_people" title="Tenggerese people">Tengger tribe</a> continue to practice Javanese-Hindu today, and live in villages on the slope of <a href="/wiki/Mount_Bromo" title="Mount Bromo">Mount Bromo</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Kebatinan</i>, also called <i>Kejawen</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGin2004719_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGin2004719-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> <i>Agama Jawa</i><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaldarola1982501_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaldarola1982501-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/Aliran_kepercayaan" title="Aliran kepercayaan">Kepercayaan</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHooker1988196_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHooker1988196-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> is a <a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">Javanese</a> religious tradition, consisting of an amalgam of <a href="/wiki/Animism" title="Animism">animistic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hindu" class="mw-redirect" title="Hindu">Hindu</a>-<a href="/wiki/Buddhist" class="mw-redirect" title="Buddhist">Buddhist</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Islamic" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic">Islamic</a>, especially <a href="/wiki/Sufi" class="mw-redirect" title="Sufi">Sufi</a>, beliefs and practices. It is rooted in Javanese history and religiosity, <a href="/wiki/Syncretism" title="Syncretism">syncretising</a> aspects of different religions.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Calendar">Calendar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Javanese_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Calendar">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Javanese_calendar" title="Javanese calendar">Javanese calendar</a></div>
<p>The Javanese <a href="/wiki/Calendar" title="Calendar">calendar</a> is used by the Javanese people concurrently with two other calendars, the <a href="/wiki/Gregorian_calendar" title="Gregorian calendar">Gregorian calendar</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_calendar" title="Islamic calendar">Islamic calendar</a>. The Gregorian calendar is the official calendar of Indonesia, while the Islamic calendar is used by Muslims and Indonesian government for religious worship and deciding relevant <a href="/wiki/Islamic_holidays" title="Islamic holidays">Islamic holidays</a>. The Javanese calendar is presently used mostly for cultural events (such as <a href="/wiki/Satu_Sura" class="mw-redirect" title="Satu Sura">Siji Surå</a>). The Javanese calendar system is currently a lunar calendar adopted by <a href="/wiki/Sultan_Agung" class="mw-redirect" title="Sultan Agung">Sultan Agung</a> in 1633, based on the Islamic calendar. Previously, Javanese people used a solar system based on the <a href="/wiki/Hindu_calendar" title="Hindu calendar">Hindu calendar</a>.
</p><p>Unlike many other calendars, the Javanese calendar uses a 5-day week known as the <i><a href="/wiki/Javanese_calendar#Five-day_week_(Pasaran)" title="Javanese calendar">Pasaran</a></i> cycle. This is still in use today and is superimposed with 7-day week of the <a href="/wiki/Gregorian_calendar" title="Gregorian calendar">Gregorian calendar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Islamic_calendar" title="Islamic calendar">Islamic calendar</a> to become what is known as the 35-day <a href="/wiki/Javanese_calendar#Wetonan_cycle" title="Javanese calendar">Wetonan cycle</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">Architecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Javanese_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Architecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Javanese_traditional_house" title="Javanese traditional house">Javanese traditional house</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:RUMAHJAWA_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/RUMAHJAWA_1.jpg/220px-RUMAHJAWA_1.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/RUMAHJAWA_1.jpg/330px-RUMAHJAWA_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/RUMAHJAWA_1.jpg/440px-RUMAHJAWA_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="750" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:RUMAHJAWA_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Traditional Javanese house.</div></div></div>
<p>Throughout their long history, the Javanese have produced many important buildings, ranging from Hindu monuments, Buddhist <a href="/wiki/Stupa" title="Stupa">stupa</a>, mortuary temples, palace complexes, and mosques.
</p><p>Two important religious monuments are the Hindu temple of <a href="/wiki/Prambanan" title="Prambanan">Prambanan</a> and the Buddhist temple of <a href="/wiki/Borobudur" title="Borobudur">Borobudur</a>. Both of them are 9th century temples and <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" title="World Heritage Site">World Heritage Sites</a>. Both are located near <a href="/wiki/Yogyakarta" title="Yogyakarta">Yogyakarta</a> in the slope of Mount Merapi.
</p><p>Meanwhile, examples of secular buildings can be seen in the ruins of the former capital city of the <a href="/wiki/Majapahit" title="Majapahit">Majapahit</a> Kingdom (14th to 16th century AD) in <a href="/wiki/Trowulan" title="Trowulan">Trowulan</a>, <a href="/wiki/East_Java" title="East Java">East Java</a>. The complex covers an area of 11&#160;km x 9&#160;km. It consists of various brick buildings, a canal ranging from 20 to 40 meters wide, purification pools, temples and iconic split gates.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> The capital complex is currently being considered as a candidate for becoming a <a href="/wiki/UNESCO_World_Heritage_Site" class="mw-redirect" title="UNESCO World Heritage Site">UNESCO World Heritage Site</a>.
</p><p>Traditional Javanese buildings can be identified by their trapezoid shaped roofs supported by wooden pillars.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> Another common feature in Javanese buildings are <i><a href="/wiki/Pendopo" class="mw-redirect" title="Pendopo">pendopo</a></i>,  pavilions with open-sides and four large pillars. The pillars and other parts of the buildings can be richly <a href="/wiki/Wood_carving" title="Wood carving">carved</a>. This architecture style can be found at <i><a href="/wiki/Kraton_(Indonesia)" title="Kraton (Indonesia)">kraton</a></i>, or palaces, of the Sultanates of <a href="/wiki/Yogyakarta" title="Yogyakarta">Yogyakarta</a> (palaces of <a href="/wiki/Hamengkubuwono" title="Hamengkubuwono">Hamengkubuwono</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pakualaman" title="Pakualaman">Pakualaman</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Surakarta" title="Surakarta">Surakarta</a> (palaces of <a href="/wiki/Pakubuwono" class="mw-redirect" title="Pakubuwono">Pakubuwono</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mangkunegaran" title="Mangkunegaran">Mangkunegaran</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Traditional <a href="/wiki/Mosque" title="Mosque">mosques</a> in Java maintain a distinctive Javanese style. The <i>pendopo</i> model is used as the main feature of mosques as prayer halls. A trapezoidal roof is used instead of the more typically Muslim <a href="/wiki/Dome" title="Dome">dome</a>. These roofs are often multi-tiered and tiled.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> In addition to not using domes, traditional Javanese mosques also often lack <a href="/wiki/Minaret" title="Minaret">minarets</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> The split gate from earlier Hindu-Buddhist period is still used in many mosques and public buildings in Java.
</p><p>Some notable examples of mosques using traditional Javanese architecture include the <a href="/wiki/Agung_Demak_Mosque" class="mw-redirect" title="Agung Demak Mosque">Agung Demak Mosque</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Menara_Kudus_Mosque" title="Menara Kudus Mosque">Menara Kudus Mosque</a> and the Grand Mosque of <a href="/wiki/Banten" title="Banten">Banten</a>. The Kudus Mosque is also of note because it incorporates Hindu-style stone architecture.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cuisine">Cuisine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Javanese_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Cuisine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Javanese_cuisine" title="Javanese cuisine">Javanese cuisine</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Javanese_Cuisine,_Djogdja.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Javanese_Cuisine%2C_Djogdja.jpg/200px-Javanese_Cuisine%2C_Djogdja.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Javanese_Cuisine%2C_Djogdja.jpg/300px-Javanese_Cuisine%2C_Djogdja.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Javanese_Cuisine%2C_Djogdja.jpg/400px-Javanese_Cuisine%2C_Djogdja.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3072" data-file-height="2304" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Javanese_Cuisine,_Djogdja.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Example of Javanese cuisine. Clockwise: fried <a href="/wiki/Tempeh" title="Tempeh">tempeh</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Gnetum_gnemon" title="Gnetum gnemon">mlinjo</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Krupuk#Indonesia" title="Krupuk">crackers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gudeg" title="Gudeg">gudeg</a> with <a href="/wiki/Rice" title="Rice">rice</a> wrapped in <a href="/wiki/Teak" title="Teak">teak</a> leaf, green <a href="/wiki/Sambal" title="Sambal">chili sambal</a> and sliced <a href="/wiki/Lime_(fruit)" title="Lime (fruit)">lime</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nasi_Tumpeng.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Nasi_Tumpeng.jpg/200px-Nasi_Tumpeng.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="133" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Nasi_Tumpeng.jpg/300px-Nasi_Tumpeng.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Nasi_Tumpeng.jpg/400px-Nasi_Tumpeng.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="681" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nasi_Tumpeng.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Nasi <a href="/wiki/Tumpeng" title="Tumpeng">tumpeng</a></i>, the quintessentially Javanese rice dish, symbolises the volcano.</div></div></div>
<p>Javanese cuisine and culture place an important role in <a href="/wiki/Rice" title="Rice">rice</a>, which is a staple food on the island. Among the Javanese it is considered not to be a meal if a person hasn't eaten rice yet.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> It is also important part of identity that differentiate Javanese with foreigners that eat <a href="/wiki/Bread" title="Bread">bread</a> (the Europeans) and resident of other island who eat <a href="/wiki/Sago" title="Sago">sago</a> (for example <a href="/wiki/Molucca" class="mw-redirect" title="Molucca">Moluccans</a>). Rice is also symbol of development and prosperity, while <a href="/wiki/Cassava" title="Cassava">cassava</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tuber" title="Tuber">tuber</a> is associated with poverty.<sup id="cite_ref-dufon_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dufon-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Javanese cuisine varies by region. Eastern Javanese cuisine has a preference for more salty and hot foods,<sup id="cite_ref-dufon_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dufon-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> while the Central Javanese prefer sweeter foods.
</p><p>A famous food in Javanese cuisine is <a href="/wiki/Rujak#Rujak_Cingur" class="mw-redirect" title="Rujak"><i>Rujak Cingur</i></a>,<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> marinated <a href="/wiki/Cow" class="mw-redirect" title="Cow">cow</a> lips and noses served with vegetable, shrimp prawn and <a href="/wiki/Peanut_sauce" title="Peanut sauce">peanut sauce</a> with <a href="/wiki/Sambal" title="Sambal">chili</a>. Rojak Cingur is considered a traditional food in <a href="/wiki/Surabaya" title="Surabaya">Surabaya</a> in <a href="/wiki/East_Java" title="East Java">East Java</a>.
</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/Gudeg" title="Gudeg">Gudeg</a></i> is a traditional food from <a href="/wiki/Yogyakarta" title="Yogyakarta">Yogyakarta</a><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Central_Java" title="Central Java">Central Java</a> which is made from young Nangka (<a href="/wiki/Jack_fruit" class="mw-redirect" title="Jack fruit">jack fruit</a>) boiled for several hours with <a href="/wiki/Palm_sugar" title="Palm sugar">palm sugar</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Coconut_milk" title="Coconut milk">coconut milk</a>.
</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/Pecel" title="Pecel">Pecel</a></i>, a type of peanut sauce with chili<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> is a common ingredient in Javanese cuisine. It is used in various types of <a href="/wiki/Rujak" class="mw-redirect" title="Rujak">Rujak</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gado-gado" title="Gado-gado">Gado-gado</a>. It can also be used as stand-alone sauce with rice, prawns, eggs and vegetables as <i>Nasi Pecel</i> (Pecel rice).<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/Tumpeng" title="Tumpeng">Tumpeng</a></i>, is a rice served in the shape of a <a href="/wiki/Stratovolcano" title="Stratovolcano">conical volcano</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> usually with rice coloured yellow using <a href="/wiki/Turmeric" title="Turmeric">turmeric</a>. It is an important part of many ceremonies in Java. Tumpeng is served at landmark events such as birthdays, moving house, or other ceremonies.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Traditionally, Tumpeng is served alongside fried chicken, boiled egg, vegetables, and goat meat on a round plate made from <a href="/wiki/Bamboo" title="Bamboo">bamboo</a> called <i>besek</i>.
</p><p>A notable food in Java is <i><a href="/wiki/Tempeh" title="Tempeh">tempeh</a></i>, a <a href="/wiki/Meat_substitute" class="mw-redirect" title="Meat substitute">meat substitute</a> made from <a href="/wiki/Soy_bean" class="mw-redirect" title="Soy bean">soy bean</a> fermented with <a href="/wiki/Mould" class="mw-redirect" title="Mould">mould</a>. It is a staple source of protein in Java and popular around the world as a meat substitute for <a href="/wiki/Vegetarian" class="mw-redirect" title="Vegetarian">vegetarians</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Names">Names</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Javanese_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Names">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Javanese_names" class="mw-redirect" title="Javanese names">Javanese names</a></div>
<p>Javanese do not usually have family names or surnames, with only a single name. Javanese names may come from traditional Javanese languages, many of which are derived from <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a>. Names with the prefix <i>Su-</i>, which means <i>good</i>, are very popular. After the advent of Islam, many Javanese began to use <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> names, especially coast populations, where Islamic influences are stronger. Commoners usually only have one-word names, while nobilities use two-or-more-word names, but rarely a surname. Some people use a <a href="/wiki/Patronymic" title="Patronymic">patronymic</a>. Due to the influence of other cultures, many people started using names from other languages, mainly <a href="/wiki/European_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="European languages">European languages</a>. <a href="/wiki/Christians" title="Christians">Christian</a> Javanese usually use <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">baptism</a> names followed by a traditional Javanese name.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Occupations">Occupations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Javanese_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Occupations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In Indonesia, Javanese people can be found in all occupations, especially in the government and the military.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Farming">Farming</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Javanese_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Farming">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Traditionally, most Javanese people are farmers. Farming is especially common because of the fertile volcanic soil in Java. The most important agricultural commodity is rice. In 1997, it was estimated that Java produced 55% of Indonesia's total output of the crop.<sup id="cite_ref-agri_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-agri-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Most farmers work in small-scale rice fields, with around 42% of farmers working and cultivating less than 0.5 hectares of land.<sup id="cite_ref-agri_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-agri-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> In region where soil is less fertile of where rainy season is short, other staple crops is cultivated, such as <a href="/wiki/Cassava" title="Cassava">cassava</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-dunham_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dunham-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Merchant-Sailor">Merchant-Sailor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Javanese_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Merchant-Sailor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Borobudur_Ship_(Leemans,_pl._ci,_172).png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Borobudur_Ship_%28Leemans%2C_pl._ci%2C_172%29.png/220px-Borobudur_Ship_%28Leemans%2C_pl._ci%2C_172%29.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="153" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Borobudur_Ship_%28Leemans%2C_pl._ci%2C_172%29.png/330px-Borobudur_Ship_%28Leemans%2C_pl._ci%2C_172%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Borobudur_Ship_%28Leemans%2C_pl._ci%2C_172%29.png/440px-Borobudur_Ship_%28Leemans%2C_pl._ci%2C_172%29.png 2x" data-file-width="867" data-file-height="603" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Borobudur_Ship_(Leemans,_pl._ci,_172).png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Borobudur ship from Borobudur temple</div></div></div>
<p>In the ancient times, the Javanese people are excelled at navigating the seas and trading. This is because not all commodities can be found at the island of Java, and trading is required to fulfill the life necessities. Javanese merchant and sailors already in frequent voyage in the seas between India and China as early as 1st century CE.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Borobudur_ship" title="Borobudur ship">Borobudur ship</a> of the Javanese <a href="/wiki/Sailendra_Dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Sailendra Dynasty">Sailendra dynasty</a> brought Nusantaran sailor and settler to <a href="/wiki/Ghana_Empire" title="Ghana Empire">Ghana</a> and <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a> in the 8th century CE,<i><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup></i> but there is possibility that they have been there as early as 500 BCE.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During the Majapahit era, almost all of the commodities from Asia can be found in Java. This is because extensive shipping by Majapahit empire using various type of ships, particularly the <a href="/wiki/Jong_(ship)" class="mw-redirect" title="Jong (ship)">jong</a>, for trading to faraway places.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> It was also during Majapahit era, Nusantaran exploration reached its greatest accomplishment. <a href="/wiki/Ludovico_di_Varthema" title="Ludovico di Varthema">Ludovico di Varthema</a> (1470-1517), in his book <i>Itinerario de Ludouico de Varthema Bolognese</i> stated that the Southern Javanese people sailed to "far Southern lands" up to the point they arrived at an island where a day only last 4 hours long and "colder than in any part of the world". Modern studies determine that such place is located at least 900 nautical miles (1666 km) southward the Southern point of <a href="/wiki/Tasmania" title="Tasmania">Tasmania</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> When Afonso de Albuquerque conquered Malacca, the Portuguese recovered a chart from a Javanese pilot, which already included part of the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:5_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>European colonial presence diminish the Javanese merchant-sailor range. However in 1602, Diogo de Couto confirms that the Javanese were still in communication with east coast of Madagascar.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> The decision of <a href="/wiki/Amangkurat_I_of_Mataram" title="Amangkurat I of Mataram">Amangkurat I</a> of <a href="/wiki/Mataram_Sultanate" title="Mataram Sultanate">Mataram Sultanate</a> to destroy ships on coastal cities and close ports to prevent them from rebelling in mid-17th century further reducing the Javanese people's ability in long distance sailing. In the second half of 18th century, most of the Javanese merchant-sailor is restricted to only short range travel.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shipbuilder">Shipbuilder</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Javanese_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Shipbuilder">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Javanese were known to produce large ships called jong. These ships already plied the seas between India and China as early as 1st century CE, carrying up to 1000 people alongside 250-1000 tons of cargo.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> The jong was mainly constructed in two major shipbuilding centres around Java: north coastal Java, especially around <a href="/wiki/Rembang_Regency" title="Rembang Regency">Rembang</a>-<a href="/wiki/Demak_Regency" title="Demak Regency">Demak</a> (along the Muria strait) and <a href="/wiki/Cirebon" title="Cirebon">Cirebon</a>; and the south coast of Borneo (<a href="/wiki/Banjarmasin" title="Banjarmasin">Banjarmasin</a>) and the adjacent islands. <a href="/wiki/Pegu" class="mw-redirect" title="Pegu">Pegu</a>, which is a large shipbuilding port at the 16th century, also produced jong, built by Javanese who resided there.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Impressed by their ability, <a href="/wiki/Afonso_de_Albuquerque" title="Afonso de Albuquerque">Albuquerque</a> hired 60 Javanese carpenters and shipbuilders to work in Malacca for the Portuguese. The shipbuilding in Java was hampered when the VOC gained foothold in Java starting in early 17th century. They prohibited the locals from building vessels more than 50 tons in tonnage and assigned European supervisors to shipyards.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> However, in the 18th century the javanese shipbuilding areas (particularly <a href="/wiki/Rembang_Regency" title="Rembang Regency">Rembang</a> and Juwana) has started builiding large European-styled vessels (<a href="/wiki/Bark_(ship)" class="mw-redirect" title="Bark (ship)">bark</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brigantine" title="Brigantine">brigantine</a> type) ranging between 160-600 tons in tonnage.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Blacksmith">Blacksmith</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Javanese_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Blacksmith">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Semar_Kris_(alt)_3.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Semar_Kris_%28alt%29_3.jpg/220px-Semar_Kris_%28alt%29_3.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Semar_Kris_%28alt%29_3.jpg/330px-Semar_Kris_%28alt%29_3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Semar_Kris_%28alt%29_3.jpg/440px-Semar_Kris_%28alt%29_3.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2958" data-file-height="1932" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Semar_Kris_(alt)_3.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A decorative kris with a figure of <a href="/wiki/Semar" title="Semar">Semar</a> as the handle. The <i>bilah</i> has thirteen <i>luk</i>.</div></div></div>
<p>Blacksmiths are traditionally valued. Some blacksmiths fast and meditate to reach perfection. Javanese blacksmiths create a range of tools and farming equipment, and also cultural items such as gamelan instruments and <a href="/wiki/Kris" title="Kris">kris</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-dunham_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dunham-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Majapahit" title="Majapahit">Majapahit</a> used fire-arms and cannonade as a feature of warfare. <a href="/wiki/Cetbang" title="Cetbang">Cetbang</a>, the Javanese bronze breech-loaded swivel-gun, was used ubiquitously by the Majapahit navy, pirates, and rival lords. The demise of the <a href="/wiki/Majapahit" title="Majapahit">Majapahit</a> empire also caused the flight of disaffected skilled bronze cannon-smiths to <a href="/wiki/Brunei" title="Brunei">Brunei</a>, modern <a href="/wiki/Sumatra" title="Sumatra">Sumatra</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>. This led to near universal use of the swivel-gun, especially on trade vessels to protect against pirates, in the <a href="/wiki/Makassar_Strait" title="Makassar Strait">Makassar Strait</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Thomas_Stamford_Raffles_1965_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas_Stamford_Raffles_1965-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Duarte Barbosa ca. 1510 said that the inhabitants of Java are great masters in casting artillery and very good artillerymen. They make many one-pounder cannons (cetbang or <a href="/wiki/Lantaka" title="Lantaka">rentaka</a>), long muskets, and other fire-works. Every place are considered excellent in casting artillery, and in the knowledge of using it.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> In 1513, the <a href="/wiki/Djong_(ship)#History" title="Djong (ship)">Javanese fleet</a> led by Patih Yunus, when sailed to attack <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Malacca" title="Portuguese Malacca">Portuguese Malacca</a> "carried much artillery made in Java, for [the Javanese] are excellent founders".<sup id="cite_ref-:02_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:02-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Kris" title="Kris">Kris</a> knives are important items, with many heirloom kris holding significant historical value. The design of the kris is to tear apart an opponent's abdomen, making the injury more severe.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Kota_Gede" class="mw-redirect" title="Kota Gede">Kota Gede</a> is famous for its <a href="/wiki/Silver" title="Silver">silverworks</a> and silver handicrafts.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Batik_making">Batik making</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Javanese_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Batik making">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Batik" title="Batik">Batiks</a> are traditionally made by women as a pastime, but some town and villages have specialised in making batik, such as Pekalongan, Kauman, Kampung Taman and Laweyan.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wood_carving">Wood carving</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Javanese_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Wood carving">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Houtsnijders_vervaardigen_maskers_Nederlands-Indi%C3%AB_TMnr_60026954.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Houtsnijders_vervaardigen_maskers_Nederlands-Indi%C3%AB_TMnr_60026954.jpg/220px-COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Houtsnijders_vervaardigen_maskers_Nederlands-Indi%C3%AB_TMnr_60026954.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Houtsnijders_vervaardigen_maskers_Nederlands-Indi%C3%AB_TMnr_60026954.jpg/330px-COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Houtsnijders_vervaardigen_maskers_Nederlands-Indi%C3%AB_TMnr_60026954.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Houtsnijders_vervaardigen_maskers_Nederlands-Indi%C3%AB_TMnr_60026954.jpg/440px-COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Houtsnijders_vervaardigen_maskers_Nederlands-Indi%C3%AB_TMnr_60026954.jpg 2x" data-file-width="700" data-file-height="527" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Houtsnijders_vervaardigen_maskers_Nederlands-Indi%C3%AB_TMnr_60026954.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Javanese woodworkers making traditional masks during the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_East_Indies" title="Dutch East Indies">Dutch East Indies</a> era.</div></div></div>
<p>The Javanese art of wood carving is traditionally applied to various cultural attributes such as statues, (wayang-)dolls, and masks. Woodcarving also prominent as house ornamentation and details. The elaborately carved Omah Kudus is a fine example of Javanese woodcarving mastery. The Central Java town of <a href="/wiki/Jepara" title="Jepara">Jepara</a> is famous as a center of Javanese woodcarving workshops, where artists and carpenters especially working on Javan <a href="/wiki/Teak" title="Teak">teak</a> wood.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Migrations">Migrations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Javanese_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Migrations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Javanese were probably involved in the Austronesian migration to <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a> in the first centuries C.E. While the culture of the migration is most closely related with the <a href="/wiki/Ma%27anyan_people" title="Ma&#39;anyan people">Ma'anyan people</a> of Borneo, a portion of the <a href="/wiki/Malagasy_language" title="Malagasy language">Malagasy language</a> is derived from loanwords from the <a href="/wiki/Javanese_language" title="Javanese language">Javanese language</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Since the Hindu kingdom period, Javanese <a href="/wiki/Merchant" title="Merchant">merchants</a> settled at many places in the Indonesian archipelago.<sup id="cite_ref-survey_28-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-survey-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>247</span></sup> In the late 15th century, following the collapse of Majapahit and the rise of Muslim principalities on the northern coast of Java, many Hindu nobilities, artisans and courtiers migrated to <a href="/wiki/Bali" title="Bali">Bali</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-handbook_29-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-handbook-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> where they would contribute to the refined culture of Bali. Others who refused to convert to Islam retreated to <a href="/wiki/Bromo_Tengger_Semeru_National_Park" title="Bromo Tengger Semeru National Park">Tengger mountain</a>, retaining their Hindu religion and becoming the <a href="/wiki/Tenggerese_people" title="Tenggerese people">Tenggerese people</a>.
</p><p>In the conflicts during the transitions of power between the <a href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_Demak" class="mw-redirect" title="Sultanate of Demak">Demak</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Pajang" title="Kingdom of Pajang">Pajang</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_Mataram" class="mw-redirect" title="Sultanate of Mataram">Mataram</a> in the late 16th century, some Javanese migrated to <a href="/wiki/Palembang" title="Palembang">Palembang</a> in southern <a href="/wiki/Sumatra" title="Sumatra">Sumatra</a>. There they established a sultanate and formed a mix of <a href="/wiki/Malay_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Malay people">Malay</a> and Javanese culture.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Palembang_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Palembang language">Palembang language</a> is a dialect of <a href="/wiki/Malay_language" title="Malay language">Malay language</a> with heavy influence of Javanese.
</p><p>During the reign of <a href="/wiki/Sultan_Agung_of_Mataram" title="Sultan Agung of Mataram">Sultan Agung</a> (1613–1645), some Javanese began to establish settlements in coastal <a href="/wiki/West_Java" title="West Java">West Java</a> around <a href="/wiki/Cirebon" title="Cirebon">Cirebon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indramayu" title="Indramayu">Indramayu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Karawang" title="Karawang">Karawang</a>. These Javanese settlements were originally commissioned by Sultan Agung as rice farming villages to support the Javanese troop logistics on his military campaign against Dutch Batavia.
</p><p>The Javanese were also present in <a href="/wiki/Peninsular_Malaysia" title="Peninsular Malaysia">Peninsular Malaya</a> since early times.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> The Link between Java and Malacca was important during <a href="/wiki/The_spread_of_Islam_in_Indonesia" class="mw-redirect" title="The spread of Islam in Indonesia">spread of Islam</a> in Indonesia, when <a href="/wiki/Ulama" title="Ulama">religious missionaries</a> were sent from Malacca to seaports on the northern coast of Java.<sup id="cite_ref-wink_31-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wink-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Large migrations to the Malay Peninsula occurred during the colonial period, mostly from Central Java to <a href="/wiki/British_Malaya" title="British Malaya">British Malaya</a>. Migration also took place from 1880 to 1930 from other parts of Java with a secondary migration Javanese from Sumatra. Those migrations were to seek a new life away from the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_East_Indies" title="Dutch East Indies">Dutch colonists</a> who ruled Indonesia at that time. Today these people live throughout Peninsular Malaysia and are mainly concentrated in parts of <a href="/wiki/Johor" title="Johor">Johor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Perak" title="Perak">Perak</a> and <a href="/wiki/Selangor" title="Selangor">Selangor</a> and cities such as <a href="/wiki/Kuala_Lumpur" title="Kuala Lumpur">Kuala Lumpur</a>. 
Today, the Javanese in Malaysia are included in <a href="/wiki/Malay_race" title="Malay race">Malay race</a> along with other native Indonesian ethnics, it is estimated 40% of all Malays in Malaysia at least has some Javanese ancestry.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>In Singapore, approximately 50-60% of <a href="/wiki/Malays_in_Singapore" class="mw-redirect" title="Malays in Singapore">its Malay population</a> have some degree of Javanese ancestry. Most of them have identified themselves as Malays, rather than Javanese.<sup id="cite_ref-KeturunanSG2_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KeturunanSG2-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Javanese merchants were also present in the <a href="/wiki/Maluku_Islands" title="Maluku Islands">Maluku Islands</a> as part of the spice trade. Following the Islamisation of Java, they spread Islam in the islands, with <a href="/wiki/Ternate" title="Ternate">Ternate</a> being a Muslim sultanate circa 1484.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Javanese merchants also converted coastal cities in <a href="/wiki/Borneo" title="Borneo">Borneo</a> to Islam.<sup id="cite_ref-lapidus_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lapidus-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> The Javanese thus played an important part in transmitting Islam from the western part to the eastern part of <a href="/wiki/List_of_islands_of_Indonesia" title="List of islands of Indonesia">the Archipelago</a> with trade based from northern coast of Java.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tropenmuseum_Royal_Tropical_Institute_Objectnumber_60006744_Javaanse_arbeiders_aangekomen_bij_de.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Tropenmuseum_Royal_Tropical_Institute_Objectnumber_60006744_Javaanse_arbeiders_aangekomen_bij_de.jpg/220px-Tropenmuseum_Royal_Tropical_Institute_Objectnumber_60006744_Javaanse_arbeiders_aangekomen_bij_de.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Tropenmuseum_Royal_Tropical_Institute_Objectnumber_60006744_Javaanse_arbeiders_aangekomen_bij_de.jpg/330px-Tropenmuseum_Royal_Tropical_Institute_Objectnumber_60006744_Javaanse_arbeiders_aangekomen_bij_de.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Tropenmuseum_Royal_Tropical_Institute_Objectnumber_60006744_Javaanse_arbeiders_aangekomen_bij_de.jpg/440px-Tropenmuseum_Royal_Tropical_Institute_Objectnumber_60006744_Javaanse_arbeiders_aangekomen_bij_de.jpg 2x" data-file-width="700" data-file-height="493" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tropenmuseum_Royal_Tropical_Institute_Objectnumber_60006744_Javaanse_arbeiders_aangekomen_bij_de.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Javanese migrant workers in <a href="/wiki/Suriname" title="Suriname">Suriname</a>, circa 1940.</div></div></div>
<p>New migration patterns emerged during colonial periods. During the rise of <a href="/wiki/Dutch_East_India_Company" title="Dutch East India Company">VOC</a> power starting in the 17th century, many Javanese were exiled, enslaved or hired as mercenaries for the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Empire" title="Dutch Empire">Dutch colonies</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ceylon" class="mw-redirect" title="Ceylon">Ceylon</a> in <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Cape_colony" class="mw-redirect" title="Cape colony">Cape colony</a> in <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>. These included princes and nobility who lost their dispute with the Company and were exiled along with their retinues. These, along with exiles from other ethnicities like <a href="/wiki/Bugis" class="mw-redirect" title="Bugis">Bugis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malay_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Malay people">Malay</a> became the <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lankan_Malay" class="mw-redirect" title="Sri Lankan Malay">Sri Lankan Malay</a><sup id="cite_ref-srilankan_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-srilankan-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Cape_Malay" class="mw-redirect" title="Cape Malay">Cape Malay</a><sup id="cite_ref-cape_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cape-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> ethnic groups respectively. Other political prisoners were transported to closer places. Prince <a href="/wiki/Diponegoro" title="Diponegoro">Diponegoro</a> and his followers were transported to <a href="/wiki/North_Sulawesi" title="North Sulawesi">North Sulawesi</a>, following his defeat in <a href="/wiki/Java_War" title="Java War">Java War</a> in the early 19th century. Their descendants are well known as <a href="/w/index.php?title=Jatons&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Jatons (page does not exist)">Jaton</a> (abbreviation of "Jawa Tondano"/Tondano Javanese).
</p><p>Major migrations started during the Dutch colonial period under <a href="/wiki/Transmigration_program" title="Transmigration program">Transmigration programs</a>. The Dutch needed many labourers for their plantations and moved many Javanese under the program as contract workers, mostly to other parts of the colony in Sumatra. They also sent Javanese workers to <a href="/wiki/Suriname" title="Suriname">Suriname</a> in South America. Today approximately 15% of the Suriname population is of Javanese ancestry.
</p><p>The Transmigration program that was created by the Dutch continued following independence. A significant Javanese population can be found in the Jabodetabek (<a href="/wiki/Greater_Jakarta" class="mw-redirect" title="Greater Jakarta">Greater Jakarta</a>) area, <a href="/wiki/Lampung" title="Lampung">Lampung</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Sumatra" title="South Sumatra">South Sumatra</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jambi" title="Jambi">Jambi</a> provinces. Several <i>paguyuban</i> (traditional community organisation) were formed by these Javanese immigrants, such as "Pujakesuma" (abbreviation of Indonesian: <i>Putra Jawa Kelahiran Sumateran</i> or Sumatra-born Javanese).
</p>
